Developer Flink Optimization and Practice of Flink Unaligned Checkpoint in Shope Optimization and Practice of Flink Unaligned Checkpoint in Shope Problems in Checkpoint 1.1 Technical problems in checkpoint It is a common problem in Flink production that the backpressure of Flink operation causes the checkpoint timeout failure seriously, and the continuous backpressure will lead to a checkpoint that fails for a long time. For example, common scenarios such as external query or write performance bottlenecks, CPU bottlenecks, and data skew during rush or peak periods will indirectly cause checkpoint failures. 1.2 Impact of continuous failure of checkpoint on business • After consuming half an hour of lag data, dev found that the consumption rate of the half an hour task was slow and could not meet expectations. He wanted to increase the task parallelism and restart to improve consumption capacity. If the checkpoint fails all the time, it needs to recover from the checkpoint half an hour ago. The data consumed in this half an hour will be consumed repeatedly, leading to the risk of resource waste and possible duplication of business data. • When consuming lag, if the tolerable failed checkpoints (the default number of tolerance CP failures is 0) is too low, the Flink job may enter an endless cycle (consuming lag causes serious backpressure on the job, serious backpressure causes checkpoint timeout failure, checkpoint failure causes job failure, and job failure causes more lag consumption), the lag can never be consumed. • Unlimited tolerance of checkpoint failure is not an elegant solution. If the tolerance times are too high: ⚀ Problems in production cannot be found in time; ⚀ Some connectors will submit data or files during checkpoint. If checkpoint fails continuously, these data or files cannot be submitted for a long time, which will cause data delay and transaction timeout. For example, Kafka Producer transaction timeout will lead to transaction failure; ⚀ Once the job is restarted, a large amount of data will be consumed repeatedly. • Business peak and promotion are similar to consumption lag, and they will encounter the same problems. 1.3 Introducing Unaligned Checkpoint Based on the above background, many users hope that the checkpoint can succeed when the Flink task has a bottleneck (serious backpressure), so the Flink community has introduced the Unaligned Checkpoint mechanism (hereinafter referred to as UC) in FLIP-76. Principle of Unaligned Checkpoint 2.1 Core idea of UC When the backpressure is serious, the timeout of the aligned checkpoint (hereinafter referred to as AC) is mainly caused by the barrier queuing in the data stream. When the backpressure is serious, the data flow is very slow, leading to the slow flow of Barrier, which eventually leads to the AC timeout. The core idea of UC is that when the data flow is very slow, Barrier can surpass the data through some mechanisms, so that Barrier can quickly overtake all the way from Source to Sink. 2.2 Task UC Process Details Assume that the current task's upstream Task parallelism is 3 and the downstream Task parallelism is 2. After the UC starts, the three InputChannels of the Task will successively receive the Barriers sent by the upstream. As shown in the figure, the gray boxes represent the data in the buffer. InputChannel-0 receives the Barrier first, and other InputChannels have not received the Barrier yet. When an InputChannel receives a Barrier, it will directly start the first phase of UC, namely the UC synchronization phase. be careful: • As long as any Barrier enters the input buffer of the Task network layer, the Task directly starts UC; • Do not wait for other InputChannel to receive the Barrier, nor do you need to process the data before the Barrier in the InputChannel. As shown in the figure below, in order to ensure data consistency, the UC synchronization phase Task cannot process data. The synchronization phase will do the following: • Barrier overtaking: Send the Barrier to the head of all ResultSubPartitions, surpassing all input&output buffers, and the Barrier can be quickly sent to downstream tasks; • Snapshot of the buffer: snapshot all the input&output buffers that exceed; • Call the snapshot State method of the operator; • Flink engine takes a snapshot of the state inside the operator. There are several considerations: • During UC, the buffer data exceeded by Barrier is directly skipped. To ensure that data is not lost, these buffers need to be written to HDFS together with the State. When recovering from the checkpoint, these data will be consumed; • In the synchronization phase, the Task cannot process data. In order to minimize the blocking time, the synchronization phase only makes a reference to the buffer and status data. The actual writing of data to HDFS will be completed asynchronously; • The last two steps of UC synchronization phase are completely consistent with AC, and the State inside the operator is snapped. After the UC synchronization phase is completed, Task continues to process data, and simultaneously starts the second phase of UC: Barrier alignment and UC asynchronous phase. The asynchronous phase writes the state and buffer of the shallow copy of the synchronous phase to HDFS. Why does UC have Barrier alignment? When the task starts UC, many InputChannels do not receive Barriers, and the Barriers of these InputChannels may have network buffers that need to be snapped before. Therefore, in the second phase of UC, all the Barriers of InputChannels need to arrive, and the buffers before the Barriers need to be snapped. It can be considered that UC needs to write three types of data to HDFS: • All input&output buffers referenced in the synchronization phase; • The state inside the operator referenced in the synchronization phase; • Buffer before other InputChannel Barriers after synchronization. In the asynchronous phase, after all the three parts of data are written, the file address is reported to the JobManager, and the UC of the current Task ends. Note: Theoretically, Barrier alignment in UC asynchronous phase will be fast. As shown in the above tasks, Barrier can quickly surpass all input&output buffers and send Barrier to downstream tasks first. Therefore, upstream tasks are similar: Barrier exceeds all upstream buffers and sends it to the current task quickly. 2.3 Problems in UC practice When any Barrier enters the input buffer of the Task network layer, the Task directly starts UC. Barrier quickly surpasses all buffers and sends them to the downstream, so UC is not affected by backpressure. Theoretically: No matter how serious the backpressure is, UC Barrier can overtake all the way, quickly flow from Source to Sink, and each task can quickly complete the snapshot. The theory is very good, but in the process of actual research and task use, we found that the UC effect was not as expected: In many scenarios, UC still fails to succeed when the backpressure on tasks is severe, leading to a large discount in UC's expected benefits; UC will significantly increase the number of files written to HDFS, affect the stability of online services, and increase the difficulty of large-scale applications; UC has some bugs. The following sections will introduce the above problems, Shopee's solutions and contributions to the community. Greatly increase UC income The Task cannot process the checkpoint during data processing. It must process the currently processed data and write the results to the OutputBufferPool before checking whether the InputChannel has received the UC Barrier. If so, UC will be started. If the Task processes a piece of data and writes the result to the OutputBufferPool for more than 10 minutes, the UC will still timeout. Generally, processing a piece of data will not be slow, but writing the results to the OutputBufferPool may be time-consuming. From the perspective of OutputBufferPool, upstream Tasks are producers and downstream Tasks are consumers. Therefore, when the downstream task has a bottleneck, the upstream task output results to the OutputBufferPool will be stuck waiting for the buffer, and UC cannot be started. To solve this problem, Flink community introduced the mechanism of reserving buffer in FLINK-14396. The solution is to check whether the OutputBufferPool has an idle buffer before the Task processes the data. If there is no idle buffer, continue to wait. The detailed process is shown in the figure below. Do not process data until there is a free buffer in the OutputBufferPool, so as to ensure that the Task can write the results to the OutputBufferPool smoothly after processing the data, and will not be stuck in the data output phase in step 5. If there is no idle buffer after optimization, the task will be stuck in step 3 waiting for the idle buffer and UC Barrier phase. In this phase, when receiving the UC Barrier, UC can be started quickly. 3.1 Processing one piece of data requires improvement of multiple buffer scenarios As shown in the figure below, since only one buffer is reserved, when processing a single piece of data requires multiple buffers, the Task may still be stuck in step 5 when processing the data and outputting the results to the OutputBufferPool, resulting in the Task being unable to process UC. For example, large single data, flatmap, window trigger, and broadcast watermark all require multiple buffer scenarios to process one data. In these scenarios, Task is stuck in the data output phase of step 5, resulting in poor UC performance. The core idea to solve this problem is how to let the Task not be stuck in step 5 but in the waiting phase of step 3. Based on the above problems, Shopee proposed an overdraft buffer in FLIP-227. The idea is: if the buffer is insufficient and the TaskManager has free network memory during data processing, the OutputBufferPool of the current Task will overdraw some buffers to TM to complete the data processing step 5. Note: OutputBufferPool can only use overdraft buffer when there is no idle buffer. So once the overdraft buffer is used, when the task enters the phase of waiting for the Barrier and the idle buffer in the third step of the next round, the task will think that there is no idle buffer in the OutputBufferPool. The task will not continue to process data until all overdraft buffers are consumed by downstream tasks and there is at least one idle buffer in the OutputBufferPool. By default, taskmanager. network. memory. max overdraft buffers per gate=5, that is, each OutputBufferPool of a Task can overdraw 5 buffers to TM. After the introduction of the overdraft buffer mechanism, when the TM network memory is sufficient, if five buffers are needed to process a piece of data, the UC will not be stuck at all. If the TM network memory is large, you can increase the parameters for more scenarios. FLINK-1.16 began to support the function of overdraft buffer. The JIRA involved include FLINK-27,522, FLINK-26,762, and Flink-27,789. 3.2 Improvement of Legacy Source There are two types of tasks from the source of data, SourceTask and Non SourceTask: SourceTasks read data from external components to Flink jobs, and non SourceTasks read data from InputChannels. The data comes from upstream Tasks. Non SourceTasks will check the OutputBufferPool before reading data from the InputChannel, and will only read when there are free buffers. If SourceTask does not check whether the OutputBufferPool has idle buffers before reading data from external components, UC will perform poorly. Flink has two sources, Legacy Source and New Source: The working mode of the new source and task is pull mode, that is, the task pulls data from the source. The working mode is similar to that of the InputChannel. The task will check that the OutputBufferPool has free buffers before pulling data from the source. The Legacy Source is a push mode, that is, the Legacy Source reads data from an external component and sends it downstream directly. When the OutputBufferPool has no idle buffer, the Legacy Source will get stuck and cannot process UC normally. However, almost all Flink jobs in our production environment still use Legacy Source. Since Legacy Source has been abandoned by Flink community and is no longer maintained, Shope has made improvements to the commonly used Legacy Source. The improvement idea is similar to the above idea: Legacy Source checks that the OutputBufferPool has free buffer, and then sends data downstream. The most commonly used FlinkKafkaConsumer in Flink is actually Legacy Source, so many Flink users in the industry are still using Legacy Source. We shared the internally improved version of Legacy Source to FLINK-26759. Greatly reduce UC risk After the above optimization, when the backpressure is serious, the scenario where UC needs multiple buffers to consume a piece of data in Legacy Source can also be successful quickly, which has reached the expected effect of some Flink users, but UC still cannot meet the standard of mass production. The main reason is that UC, compared with AC, will write the network buffer to the Checkpoint, so it introduces some additional risks: More files will be written to HDFS, causing additional pressure on the NameNode; After the schema of the data is upgraded, if the serialization is incompatible, the data cannot be recovered; When the connection between operators changes, the buffer data between operators cannot be recovered (for example, changing from rebalance to forward). 4.1 Unable to switch from AC to UC smoothly Users want to avoid these risks and enjoy the benefits brought by UC, so Flink community has introduced the aligned checkpoint timeout mechanism, that is, the default checkpoint is AC, and if the AC cannot be completed within the specified time, it will be switched to UC. After the introduction of AC timeout mechanism, the risk of UC has not been completely avoided, but it is still AC without backpressure in the task, and there is no additional risk. When AC fails due to severe back pressure, switch to UC to ensure that the checkpoint can succeed. We assume that AC timeout=1min and Checkpoint timeout=5min, that is, the checkpoint still starts with AC, if AC fails for one minute, it will be switched to UC, and if the total checkpoint duration exceeds 5min, it will fail to timeout. There are three stages in the development of AC timeout. The first two stages fail to meet the expected goals, that is, when the time is up to one minute, the Job still cannot switch from AC to UC, or even five minutes cannot switch to UC, resulting in the Checkpoint timeout failure. We can understand these three stages with goals. 4.2 InputChannel supports switching from AC to UC FLINK-19680 supports the AC timeout mechanism for the first time. The principle of the first phase is that each task starts timing from receiving the first barrier. If the AC barrier alignment time in the task exceeds the AC timeout, the current task switches from AC to UC. The problem with this mechanism is that when there are many tasks in a job, there are 10 tasks from the Source to the Sink. Assuming that the internal Barrier alignment time of 10 Tasks is 59 seconds, all Tasks will not switch to UC, but all 10 Tasks need to be aligned. The total checkpoint time needs at least 590 seconds (more than 5 minutes), so the checkpoint still fails to timeout. Based on the problem in Phase 1, FLINK-23041 has made improvements. The principle of Phase 2 is that the Barrier carries the timestamp of the beginning of the checkpoint. After the InputChannel receives the Barrier, it uses the current system time minus the time of the beginning of the checkpoint to indicate how long the checkpoint has passed: If it has been more than 1 minute, directly switch to UC; If it is less than 1 minute, subtract the time consumed by AC from 1 minute to indicate how long you want to switch to UC. Set a timer. When the time is up, it will switch to UC. Compared with Phase I, Phase II solves the problem of time accumulation of multiple tasks. As long as the InputChannel receives the Barrier and the AC is not completed within the specified time, the AC can be switched to UC regularly. 4.3 Output buffer supports switching from AC to UC After Phase II is completed, it can be considered that InputChannel has well supported AC switching to UC. However, the problem is also obvious: the output buffer does not support switching from AC to UC. If the backpressure of the task is serious, the Barrier will queue in the output buffer. If the Barrier cannot send to the InputChannel of the downstream task within 5 minutes, the checkpoint will still timeout. Based on this problem, Shopee proposed an improvement in FLINK-27251 and FLINK-28077 to support the switch of output buffer from AC to UC. The design idea is: If UC is enabled and AC is current, the Barrier is sent to the end of the output buffer. But after a while, AC may need to be converted to UC, so you need to set a timer. If the Barrier is still queuing in the output buffer when the timer time is up, the AC will be converted to UC: Barrier overtaking to the output buffer header, and the light blue buffer exceeded in the figure needs to be written to the checkpoint by the snapshot. The community designed a Benchmark for Checkpoint in the early stage to evaluate the performance of Checkpoint, as shown in the figure below. The UC performance has been improved by 11 times after the optimization merge is transferred to the branch of Flink master. 4.4 UC Small File Consolidation After the AC timeout mechanism is enabled, Flink can use AC when the backpressure is not serious, and switch to UC smoothly when the backpressure is serious. It greatly reduces the additional risks of UC, and can also enjoy the benefits of UC when the backpressure is serious. But in large-scale production, there are still risks. By default, each subtask of Flink writes a file for the buffer. Assuming that there are 10 tasks and each task is concurrent at 1000, UC may write 10000 additional small files. If the Kafka cluster fails or has a bottleneck, a large number of Flink jobs are slow to write to Kafka, which will cause a large number of Flink tasks to switch from AC to UC. In this case, a large number of tasks write hundreds of thousands of small files to HDFS instantly, which may cause the NameNode avalanche. In order to solve the problem of small files, Shopee proposed an improvement to merge UC small files in FLINK-26803 and FLINK-28474. Optimization idea: multiple tasks share the same file. Each Task no longer creates files separately, but obtains file streams from CheckpointStreamManager. CheckpointStreamManager will allocate one file for n Tasks. By default, channel state. number of tasks share file=5, that is, if 5 Tasks share one UC file, the number of UC files will be reduced by 5 times. Multiple Tasks writing the same file at the same time will cause thread safety problems. Therefore, when writing files, lock the file stream to ensure that multiple Tasks write files serially. From the production experience, a large number of UC small files are within 1MB, so it is acceptable for 20 Tasks to share a file. Of course, if NN pressure is very low and Flink Job is more committed to writing efficiency, you can set this parameter to 1, which means that Tasks do not share UC files. Currently, we are still contributing to the community with the UC small file merge function. 4.5 Repair network buffer deadlock Shopee's contribution to UC also includes solving the deadlock problem when recycling network buffers in FLINK-22946. UC's production practice and future planning in Shopee 5.1 UC production practice In order to avoid the additional risks brought by UC, Shope internally sets the aligned checkpoint timeout to 1 minute, which means that the backpressure of the task is not serious. If the AC can be completed within 1 minute, then use the AC. When the backpressure is serious and AC cannot be completed within 1 minute, it will be switched to UC. The development page of the Shopee Flink platform also adds a UC switch. The user can choose whether to enable the Unaligned Checkpoint for the job. Up to now, hundreds of Flink tasks have enabled UC, and the current operations using UC perform well. UC can also succeed in backpressure. 5.2 Future planning of UC We will continue to pay attention to the problems users encounter with UC. After a few months of stable operation, we can consider enabling UC for all tasks on the premise of enabling AC timeout. The internal version of Shopee has greatly changed Flink scheduling and network memory modules. It can accurately calculate the network memory required by TM. In the future, it will reserve separate memory for UC overhead buffer. Dismiss Skip to Main Content Filter: All English Contact us AWS Marketplace Support My account More Search Filter: All Sign in to console Create account AWS Blogs AWS Big Data Blog Optimize checkpointing in your Amazon Managed Service for Apache Flink applications with buffer debloating and unaligned checkpoints – Part 2 by Lorenzo Nicora and Francisco Morillo on 14 SEP 2023 in Advanced (300), Amazon Managed Service for Apache Flink, Analytics, Thought Leadership Permalink Comments Share https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/big-data/optimize-checkpointing-in-your-amazon-managed-service-for-apache-flink-applications-with-buffer-debloating-and-unaligned-checkpoints-part-2/ February 2024: This post was reviewed and updated for accuracy. This post is a continuation of a two-part series. In the first part, we delved into Apache Flink's internal mechanisms for checkpointing, in-flight data buffering, and handling backpressure. We covered these concepts in order to understand how buffer debloating and unaligned checkpoints allow us to enhance performance for specific conditions in Apache Flink applications. In Part 1, we introduced and examined how to use buffer debloating to improve in-flight data processing. In this post, we focus on unaligned checkpoints. This feature has been available since Apache Flink 1.11 and has received many improvements since then. Unaligned checkpoints help, under specific conditions, to reduce checkpointing time for applications suffering temporary backpressure, and can be now enabled in Amazon Managed Service for Apache Flink applications running Apache Flink 1.15.2 through a support ticket. Even though this feature might improve performance for your checkpoints, if your application is constantly failing because of checkpoints timing out, or is suffering from having constant backpressure, you may require a deeper analysis and redesign of your application. Aligned checkpoints As discussed in Part 1, Apache Flink checkpointing allows applications to record state in case of failure. We've already discussed how checkpoints, when triggered by the job manager, signal all source operators to snapshot their state, which is then broadcasted as a special record called a checkpoint barrier. This process achieves exactly-once consistency for state in a distributed streaming application through the alignment of these barriers. Let's walk through the process of aligned checkpoints in a standard Apache Flink application. Remember that Apache Flink distributes the workload horizontally: each operator (a node in the logical flow of your application, including sources and sinks) is split into multiple sub-tasks based on its parallelism. Barrier alignment The alignment of checkpoint barriers is crucial for achieving exactly-once consistency in Apache Flink applications during checkpoint runs. To recap, when a job manager triggers a checkpoint, all sub-tasks of source operators receive a signal to initiate the checkpoint process. Each sub-task independently snapshots its state to the state backend and broadcasts a special record known as a checkpoint barrier to all outgoing streams. When an application operates with a parallelism higher than 1, multiple instances of each task—referred to as sub-tasks—enable parallel message consumption and processing. A sub-task can receive distinct partitions of the same stream from different upstream sub-tasks, such as after a stream repartitioning with keyBy or rebalance operations. To maintain exactly-once consistency, all sub-tasks must wait for the arrival of all checkpoint barriers before taking a snapshot of the state. The following diagram illustrates the checkpoint barriers flow. This phase is called checkpoint alignment. During alignment, the sub-task stops processing records from the partitions from which it has already received barriers, as shown in the following figure. However, it continues to process partitions that are behind the barrier. When barriers from all upstream partitions have arrived, the sub-task takes a snapshot of its state. Then it broadcasts the barrier downstream. The time a sub-task spends waiting for all barriers to arrive is measured by the checkpoint Alignment Duration metric, which can be observed in the Apache Flink UI. If the application experiences backpressure, an increase in this metric could lead to longer checkpoint durations and even checkpoint failures due to timeouts. This is where unaligned checkpoints become a viable option to potentially enhance checkpointing performance. Unaligned checkpoints Unaligned checkpoints address situations where backpressure is not just a temporary spike, but results in timeouts for aligned checkpoints, due to barrier queuing within the stream. As discussed in Part 1, checkpoint barriers can't overtake regular records. Therefore, significant backpressure can slow down the movement of barriers across the application, potentially causing checkpoint timeouts. The objective of unaligned checkpoints is to enable barrier overtaking, allowing barriers to move swiftly from source to sink even when the data flow is slower than anticipated. Building on what we saw in Part 1 concerning checkpoints and what aligned checkpoints are, let's explore how unaligned checkpoints modify the checkpointing mechanism. Upon emission, each source's checkpoint barrier is injected into the stream flowing across sub-tasks. It travels from the source output network buffer queue into the input network buffer queue of the subsequent operator. Upon the arrival of the first barrier in the input network buffer queue, the operator initially waits for barrier alignment. If the specified alignment timeout expires because not all barriers have reached the end of the input network buffer queue, the operator switches to unaligned checkpoint mode. The alignment timeout can be set programmatically by env.getCheckpointConfig().setAlignedCheckpointTimeout(Duration.ofSeconds(30)) , but modifying the default is not recommended in Apache Flink 1.15. The operator waits until all checkpoint barriers are present in the input network buffer queue before triggering the checkpoint. Unlike aligned checkpoints, the operator doesn't need to wait for all barriers to reach the queue's end, allowing the operator to have in-flight data from the buffer that hasn't been processed before checkpoint initiation. After all barriers have arrived in the input network buffer queue, the operator advances the barrier to the end of the output network buffer queue. This enhances checkpointing speed because the barrier can smoothly traverse the application from source to sink, independent of the application's end-to-end latency. After forwarding the barrier to the output network buffer queue, the operator initiates the snapshot of in-flight data between the barriers in the input and output network buffer queues, along with the snapshot of the state. Although processing is momentarily paused during this process, the actual writing to the remote persistent state storage occurs asynchronously, preventing potential bottlenecks. The local snapshot, encompassing in-flight messages and state, is saved asynchronously in the remote persistent state store, while the barrier continues its journey through the application. When to use unaligned checkpoints Unaligned checkpoints help only when barrier alignment has a significant impact on checkpoint duration. You should consider enabling this feature only when Alignment Duration is higher than the sum of Sync Duration and Async Duration. The contributors to checkpoint duration can be observed in the Flink Dashboard. A common scenario when barrier alignment may slow down checkpoints and unaligned checkpoints may help, is when temporary backpressure arises. Typical causes of temporary backpressure are: A surge in data ingestion Backfilling or catching up with historical data Increased message processing time due to delayed external systems Additionally, unaligned checkpoints are advantageous in scenarios involving exactly-once sinks. End-to-end latency can be reduced by accelerating the two-phase commit utilized by the sink. When not to use unaligned checkpoints Unaligned checkpoints won't reduce the time required for savepoints (called snapshots in the Amazon Managed Service for Apache Flink implementation) because savepoints exclusively utilize aligned checkpoints. Furthermore, because Apache Flink doesn't permit concurrent unaligned checkpoints, savepoints won't occur simultaneously with unaligned checkpoints, potentially elongating savepoint durations. Unaligned checkpoints won't fix any underlying issue in your application design. If your application is suffering from persistent backpressure or constant checkpointing timeouts, this might indicate data skewness or underprovisioning, which may require improving and tuning the application. Using unaligned checkpoints with buffer debloating One alternative for reducing the risks associated with an increased state size is to combine unaligned checkpoints with buffer debloating. This approach results in having less in-flight data to snapshot and store in the state, along with less data to be used for recovery in case of failure. This synergy facilitates enhanced performance and efficient checkpoint runs, leading to smaller checkpointing sizes and faster recovery times. When testing the use of unaligned checkpoints, we recommend doing so with buffer debloating to prevent the state size from increasing. Limitations Unaligned checkpoints are subject to the following limitations: They provide no benefit for operators with a parallelism of 1. They only improve performance for operators where barrier alignment would have occurred.Barrier alignment only happens after a repartitioning, a keyBy operation, or when an operator receives input from multiple sources, like joins. Although checkpoint barriers can surpass records in the network's buffer queue, this won't occur if the sub-task is currently processing a message. If processing a message takes too much time (for example, a flat-map operation emitting numerous records for each input record), barrier handling will be delayed. As we have seen, savepoints always use aligned checkpoints. If the savepoints of your applications are slow due to barrier alignment, unaligned checkpoints will not help. Additional limitations affect watermarks, message ordering, and broadcast state in recovery. For more details, refer to Limitations. Considerations Considerations before enabling unaligned checkpoints: Unaligned checkpoints introduce additional I/O to checkpoint storage. Checkpoints encompass not only operator state but also in-flight data within network buffer queues, leading to increased state size. These factors will increase checkpoint size and Async Duration. If your application already has significantly large checkpoint size or Async Duration, unaligned checkpoints may detrimentally impact performance. Recommendations We offer the following recommendations: Consider enabling unaligned checkpoints only if both of the following conditions are true: Checkpoints are timing out. Consider enabling buffer debloating first, and evaluate whether it solves the problem of checkpoints timing out. If buffer debloating doesn't help and the average checkpoint Alignment Duration of any operator is greater than the sum of Sync Duration and Async Duration, consider enabling unaligned checkpoints along with buffer debloating. Buffer debloating mitigates the drawbacks of unaligned checkpoints, reducing the amount of in-flight data. If unaligned checkpoints and buffer debloating together don't improve checkpoint alignment duration, consider testing unaligned checkpoints alone. Finally, but most importantly, always test unaligned checkpoints in a non-production environment first, running some comparative performance testing with a realistic workload, and verify that unaligned checkpoints actually reduce checkpoint duration. Conclusion This two-part series explored advanced strategies for optimizing checkpointing within your Amazon Managed Service for Apache Flink applications. By harnessing the potential of buffer debloating and unaligned checkpoints, you can unlock significant performance improvements and streamline checkpoint processes. However, it's important to understand when these techniques will provide improvements and when they will not. If you believe your application may benefit from checkpoint performance improvement, you can enable these features in your Amazon Managed Service For Apache Flink version 1.15 applications. We recommend first enabling buffer debloating and testing the application. If you are still not seeing the expected outcome, enable buffer debloating with unaligned checkpoints. This way, you can immediately reduce the state size and the additional I/O to state backends. Lastly, you may try using unaligned checkpoints by itself, bearing in mind the considerations we've mentioned. With a deeper understanding of these techniques and their applicability, you are better equipped to maximize the efficiency of checkpoints and mitigate the effect of backpressure in your Apache Flink application. About the Authors Lorenzo Nicora works as Senior Streaming Solution Architect helping customers across EMEA. He has been building cloud-native, data-intensive systems for over 25 years, working in the finance industry both through consultancies and for FinTech product companies. He has leveraged open-source technologies extensively and contributed to several projects, including Apache Flink. Francisco Morillo is a Streaming Solutions Architect at AWS. Francisco works with AWS customers helping them design real-time analytics architectures using AWS services, supporting Amazon Managed Streaming for Apache Kafka (Amazon MSK) and AWS's managed offering for Apache Flink. PostgreSQL Compatibility | RisingWave RisingWave Cloud V2 is now in Public Preview — Console, CLI, and native AI agent support Read More -> [ Products ] [ Use Cases ] [ Learn ] [ Pricing ] [ Customers ] [ Contact ] 9.1K Slack Log In Start Free Products Products RisingWave A complete platform for stream ingestion, processing, management, and low-latency serving RisingWave Ultra The fine-tuned edition delivering in-memory speed for ultra-low-latency and performance-critical workloads RisingWave Open Lake Managed Iceberg tables optimized for streaming data, with PostgreSQL simplicity Key Capabilities Real-Time Event Processing Gain timely insights from real-time data using SQL Incremental Materialized Views Build continuously updated views on top of your operational data Low-Latency Serving Access real-time data with single-digit millisecond latency via SQL Apache Iceberg™ Maintenance Optimize Iceberg tables with automated compaction, schema evolution, and small-file handling Comparison vs. Apache Kafka vs. Apache Flink vs. Apache Spark Streaming vs. KsqlDB vs. ClickHouse vs. Debezium Use Cases By Industry Capital Markets Financial Services Sports Betting Energy Manufacturing E-Commerce Logistics Healthcare AD/Marketing Tech By Use Case Monitoring and Alerting Real-time Data Enrichment IoT and Telemetry Pipelines Streaming Lakehouse View All Use Cases Learn Live Demos Explore interactive streaming SQL demos across 8 industries Documentation The official user documentation of RisingWave Blog Discover new perspectives and insights Videos Learn more through our videos Community Discover the impact you can have as a member Pricing Customers Contact Log In Start Free 9.1K Slack Back to Glossary Table of Contents PostgreSQL Compatibility Key Aspects of PostgreSQL Compatibility Why is PostgreSQL Compatibility Important for RisingWave? Limitations and Differences Related Blog Posts Frequently Asked Questions Related Glossary Terms PostgreSQL Compatibility PostgreSQL Compatibility for a database system like RisingWave means that it is designed to understand and interact using the protocols, SQL dialect, and data types commonly associated with PostgreSQL. This significantly lowers the barrier to adoption for users already familiar with PostgreSQL and allows integration with the vast ecosystem of tools and applications built for it. Key Aspects of PostgreSQL Compatibility Wire Protocol Compatibility: RisingWave implements the PostgreSQL wire protocol (version 3.0). Benefit: This allows standard PostgreSQL clients (like psql, pgAdmin), database drivers (JDBC, ODBC, Python's psycopg2, Node.js pg, etc.), and BI tools (Tableau, Metabase, Grafana with PostgreSQL data source) to connect to and interact with RisingWave as if it were a PostgreSQL server. Users can often use their existing tools with minimal to no changes. SQL Dialect Resemblance: RisingWave's SQL syntax for defining sources, sinks, materialized views, and querying data is heavily inspired by and largely compatible with PostgreSQL's SQL dialect. Benefit: Users familiar with PostgreSQL SQL can leverage their existing knowledge to write streaming queries in RisingWave. While RisingWave introduces streaming-specific constructs (like CREATE SOURCE, CREATE SINK, windowing functions for streaming), the core query syntax (SELECT, WHERE, GROUP BY, JOINs, etc.) feels very familiar. Data Type Support: RisingWave supports a wide range of data types that are equivalent or very similar to those found in PostgreSQL (e.g., INTEGER, VARCHAR, TIMESTAMP, DECIMAL, BOOLEAN, JSONB, arrays). Benefit: Simplifies data integration and reduces the need for complex type casting when moving data between PostgreSQL (or PostgreSQL-compatible systems) and RisingWave, or when defining schemas for data from PostgreSQL sources. System Catalog (Partial Resemblance): While not identical, RisingWave provides system catalog tables and views (e.g., in rw_catalog) that are conceptually similar to PostgreSQL's pg_catalog. This allows users and tools to introspect system metadata. Benefit: Tools that rely on querying system catalogs for schema information or object discovery can often be adapted to work with RisingWave. Why is PostgreSQL Compatibility Important for RisingWave? Reduced Learning Curve: Users don't need to learn an entirely new query language or a new set of tools to start using RisingWave for stream processing. Ecosystem Leverage: Enables immediate integration with a rich ecosystem of client libraries, drivers, ORMs, BI tools, and administrative tools. Simplified Migration/Integration: Makes it easier to build streaming applications that complement existing PostgreSQL databases or to migrate certain analytical workloads from PostgreSQL to a streaming context. Developer Productivity: Developers can use familiar tools and SQL patterns, speeding up the development of real-time data applications. Community & Knowledge Base: Users can draw upon the vast amount of existing PostgreSQL knowledge and community resources for SQL-related queries and best practices. Limitations and Differences While RisingWave strives for high compatibility, it's important to note: Streaming Focus: RisingWave is a stream processing system, not a general-purpose OLTP relational database like PostgreSQL. Its primary goal is to process and analyze data in motion. Unsupported PostgreSQL Features: Not all PostgreSQL features are supported, especially those specific to OLTP workloads, complex transactional models beyond what's needed for streaming consistency, or certain advanced SQL functions not relevant to streaming. For example, stored procedures in the traditional PostgreSQL sense are not a primary feature. Streaming-Specific SQL Extensions: RisingWave extends SQL with constructs necessary for stream processing (e.g., defining sources with connector properties, time window functions, materialized view semantics for continuous updates). DDL/DML Differences: While querying ( SELECT) is highly compatible, Data Definition Language (DDL) and Data Manipulation Language (DML) for managing tables and views in RisingWave are adapted for its streaming nature (e.g., CREATE MATERIALIZED VIEW defines a continuously updated view, not a one-time snapshot). Direct UPDATE or DELETE on base tables or sources is typically not how one interacts with streaming data; changes flow from sources. Despite these differences, the high degree of PostgreSQL compatibility is a significant advantage of RisingWave, making it more accessible and easier to integrate into existing data stacks. Related Blog Posts [ blog ] Continuous E-Commerce Data Pipeline with PostgreSQL CDC and RisingWave Cloud 7 min read [ blog ] Accessing RisingWave Data in PostgreSQL 9 min read [ blog ] RisingWave Feature: Automatic Schema Change for PostgreSQL 4 min read Frequently Asked Questions Which JetBrains IDEs do data engineers use for SQL and data pipeline development? Several data engineers shared they rely on DataGrip as their main IDE for SQL work—connecting effortlessly to MSSQL and MySQL. Others compliment PyCharm for Python tasks and IntelliJ for Scala development. Many mention using all three depending on the language or task at hand. Source: Reliability and Processing Ecosystems in Stateful Stream Processing: An Architectural and Comparative Analysis of Apache Flink and RisingWave
Type: Pasted Text
Reliability and Processing Ecosystems in Stateful Stream Processing: An Architectural and Comparative Analysis of Apache Flink and RisingWave5.2.3. Reliability and Processing Ecosystem: Processing, Compute, and ConsistencyThe development of modern stream processing architectures is shaped by the need to balance real-time processing velocity, compute resource utilization, and state consistency guarantees. Within a stateful processing ecosystem, reliability is not a single feature but an emergent property resulting from the tight integration of the processing engine, the underlying compute architecture, and the consistency protocols. The architectural paradigm chosen by a system—whether a coupled-compute-and-storage framework or a decoupled, cloud-native streaming database—defines how it manages active state, handles resource scaling, and recovers from failures.[1, 2]Historically, distributed stream processing engines prioritized sub-millisecond execution velocities by keeping application state in local memory or on disks directly attached to the compute nodes.[2, 3, 4] While this coupling minimizes the latency of state reads and writes, it introduces significant operational challenges when scaling compute resources or recovering from node failures.[2, 5]Conversely, cloud-native streaming databases separate compute from storage by leveraging remote object storage as the single source of truth.[6, 7, 8] This disaggregation of concerns allows independent scaling and simplifies failure recovery, but it requires caching and compaction strategies to mitigate the latency of remote network access.[9, 10]At the core of both paradigms is the consistency model, which guarantees that despite network partitions, hardware crashes, or upstream backpressure, the computed results remain correct and complete.[1, 3, 11] To evaluate these systems, it is necessary to examine how exactly-once semantics are maintained across different execution runtimes and storage architectures.
5.2.3.1. Exactly-Once Processing: Delivery, Correctness, and DataExactly-once processing guarantees that every record ingested from an input stream affects the internal application state and downstream sinks exactly once, even during system failures.[12, 13, 14] Achieving this degree of correctness is an end-to-end challenge that requires coordination across three distinct phases of the data pipeline: source ingest, processing, and sink commit.[12]If any phase fails to coordinate with the others, the system defaults to either at-least-once delivery (potentially causing downstream data duplication) or at-most-once delivery (risking silent data loss).[12, 13] For mission-critical applications such as financial transaction systems, ledger reconciliations, and regulatory compliance auditing, any deviation from exactly-once processing can lead to incorrect results and business disruption.[12]5.2.3.1.1. Source Actionability: Checkpoint, Replay, and LineageTo support exactly-once processing, a data source must be actionable, meaning it can participate in the processing engine's coordinated recovery protocols.[12, 16] Actionability requires that the source stream is re-readable, allowing the processing engine to reset its consumption positions and replay historical data after a failure.[12, 17]This capability is typically provided by durable, append-only logs such as Apache Kafka, which organize records into ordered partitions and track consumer progress using offset coordinates.[12, 13, 18] During normal operation, the streaming engine continuously tracks these partition offsets, persisting them as part of its operator state.[12, 16]The engine maintains a record of the stream lineage, mapping the precise sequence of source messages to the corresponding updates in downstream state operators.[12, 17] When a node fails, the processing engine halts the dataflow, rolls back the entire operator topology to the state recorded in the last successful checkpoint, and instructs the source connector to seek back to the offsets saved during that snapshot.[12, 15, 16]This deterministic replay ensures that all records processed between the last checkpoint and the time of failure are re-read and reprocessed.[15] Any state changes generated during this replay phase are reconciled with the restored checkpoint state, maintaining consistency across the stream lineage.[12]5.2.3.1.2. Producer Idempotence: Tracking, Duplication, and TokensAt the transport layer, network retries present a major challenge to message correctness. If a producer sends a record to a message broker but fails to receive an acknowledgment due to a network partition, it must retry the write.[12, 15] Without tracking, this retry can write a duplicate copy of the record to the broker.[12, 13]To prevent this duplication, modern streaming systems use producer idempotence, where the broker identifies and discards duplicate write requests.[12, 18] In Apache Kafka, for example, producer idempotence is enabled by setting enable.idempotence to true, which triggers strict validation checks across the client and broker.[18]The mechanics of this protocol rely on tracking tokens and sequence numbers.[18] During initialization, the broker assigns each producer a unique Producer ID (PID) and an epoch number.[12, 18] The producer tags every message sent to a specific topic partition with a sequentially incrementing sequence number, which serves as a unique tracking token for that payload.[18]The broker maintains the latest sequence numbers for each partition, PID, and epoch combination.[18] When a message arrives, the broker evaluates the sequence number against its local state:If the sequence number is exactly equal to the last recorded sequence number plus one (Seq_{new} = Seq_{last} + 1), the broker appends the message to the log and increments its expected sequence count.[18]If the sequence number is less than or equal to the last recorded sequence number (Seq_{new} \le Seq_{last}), the broker identifies the message as a duplicate, sends an acknowledgment to the producer, and discards the redundant payload.[12, 18]If the sequence number is greater than the last recorded sequence number plus one (Seq_{new} > Seq_{last} + 1), the broker detects an out-of-sequence write and throws an OutOfOrderSequenceException, halting the producer to protect partition integrity.[18]To ensure this tracking remains consistent across broker restarts or partition reassignments, brokers write these state parameters to active .snapshot files during log segment turnovers.[18] Upon recovery, the broker initializes its tracking state directly from these .snapshot files and the active log segments, preserving producer idempotency without requiring a connection reset.[18]This transport-level idempotency is physically constrained to a single producer connection and a single partition.[18] To guarantee correctness across multiple partitions and sessions, the system must use transactional producers that coordinate writes using transactional tokens and atomic commit protocols.[12, 13, 18]5.2.3.1.3. Atomic Commit Mechanism: Transactions, States, and FinalityWhen a stateful stream processor consumes records from multiple source partitions and writes to multiple destination partitions, it must ensure that these operations succeed or fail as a single atomic unit.[12, 13, 18] This atomicity is maintained by an atomic commit mechanism coordinated by a TransactionCoordinator.[18]The coordinator manages the transaction state machine by recording transitions inside a compacted internal system topic, such as Kafka's __transaction_state.[18] The coordinator moves transactions through several key states to ensure finality:During the initial phase, the processor reads records, executes stateful transitions, and writes the output records to the destination topics within an active transaction.[12, 13] It also writes the consumer offsets to the consumer offsets topic as part of the same transaction, linking consumption progress directly to output production.[13, 18]Once the processing epoch completes, the producer initiates the commit sequence.[18] The coordinator writes a PrepareCommit state marker to its transaction log, indicating that all participant brokers must prepare to commit their local transaction logs.[18]Once the coordinator confirms that all participants have acknowledged this transition, it writes a final Commit marker to the transaction log.[18] It then asynchronously writes commit markers to the physical data partitions, making the written records visible to downstream consumers configured with a read_committed isolation level.[13, 18]This design avoids the latency of synchronous distributed consensus, relying instead on continuous retries to achieve eventual consistency across all participant brokers.[18] If any stage of the pre-commit phase fails, or if a timeout is reached, the coordinator transitions the transaction to the Aborted state and writes abort markers to all partitions, rolling back the staged writes.[18]
5.2.3.2. Engine Comparison: Frameworks, Compute, and VelocityThe architectural trade-offs between Apache Flink and RisingWave reflect their differing design philosophies: Flink is built as a highly flexible, general-purpose stream processing framework, whereas RisingWave is designed as an integrated, SQL-native streaming database.[1, 2, 19] These differing designs affect how they manage compute and storage resources, process stateful operations, and execute query logic.[1, 2, 6]Flink utilizes a big-data style architecture that couples compute and storage within its TaskManagers.[2] During execution, Flink partitions the dataflow graph and schedules parallel tasks across these TaskManagers.[2, 22]State is kept locally in memory or on local disks attached directly to the TaskManager processes, maximizing state read and write performance.[6, 16, 23] However, if a TaskManager runs out of memory or disk space, the entire job must be halted, reconfigured, and restarted from a savepoint, limiting its scaling flexibility.[2, 5]In contrast, RisingWave adopts a cloud-native, compute-storage separated architecture designed for deployment in elastic environments.[2, 10, 24] RisingWave separates its services into independent layers: Serving Nodes handle query planning, stateless Compute Nodes run the streaming operators, Compactor Nodes manage state compaction, and cloud-native object storage holds all persistent data.[2, 24, 25]Because the compute nodes are stateless, they can be scaled up or down without requiring physical state files to be copied or redistributed.[2, 5] This disaggregated model reduces operational overhead, but it requires caching and compaction strategies to maintain low-latency access over remote networks.[9, 10]
5.2.3.2.1. Apache Flink: Streaming, Stateful, and Low-LatencyApache Flink is designed as a distributed, stateful processing framework optimized for high-throughput and low-latency execution.[1, 16, 23] By keeping active state in the local memory of TaskManagers, Flink minimizes the latency of state lookups, supporting low-latency streaming applications.[3, 16, 23]5.2.3.2.1.1. Chandy-Lamport Framework: Checkpoints, Asynchronous, and SnapshotsFlink's fault tolerance is built on distributed, periodic checkpoints generated via an implementation of the Chandy-Lamport algorithm called Asynchronous Barrier Snapshotting (ABS).[3, 5, 16] The JobManager orchestrates the checkpointing lifecycle by injecting synchronization barriers into the source operator streams.[3, 16] These barriers flow alongside regular data records, separating the stream into distinct processing epochs.[3, 17]When an operator with multiple input channels receives a barrier from one channel, it enters the barrier alignment phase.[3, 11] It pauses consumption on that channel and continues processing records from other channels until their barriers arrive.[3]During this alignment phase, incoming records on the paused channel are buffered in memory to prevent post-barrier events from modifying the operator's state before the snapshot is taken.[11] Once barriers have arrived from all input channels, the operator performs a synchronous state snapshot, forwards the barrier downstream, and unblocks the paused channels.[3, 11]Under heavy downstream backpressure, standard barrier alignment can cause issues.[3, 11] Because barriers must travel in-line with regular events, they can get delayed behind queued records in network buffers.[3, 22] This delay extends the alignment phase, occasionally causing checkpoints to time out and degrade system stability.[3, 22, 26]To address this, Flink supports unaligned checkpoints.[3, 22] When unaligned checkpointing is enabled, an operator does not wait for barrier alignment.[3, 22] Instead, it immediately snapshots its state upon receiving the first barrier on any input channel.[3, 22]It also captures all in-flight records currently sitting in its input and output network buffers, incorporating them directly into the checkpoint payload.[3, 22] This allows the barriers to overtake regular data records, ensuring checkpoints can complete even under heavy backpressure.[3, 22]The trade-off is an increase in checkpoint size, as the buffered network packets must be written to durable storage, which can be managed by combining unaligned checkpoints with buffer debloating to dynamically shrink network buffer sizes.[3, 22]5.2.3.2.1.2. RocksDB State Backend: Memory, Disk, and Out-of-Core ExecutionFor streaming applications with large state requirements, Flink uses the EmbeddedRocksDBStateBackend (which replaced the legacy RocksDBStateBackend in Flink 1.13).[3, 16] RocksDB is an embedded, out-of-core, key-value storage engine written in C++ that runs in native threads within the Flink TaskManager JVM process.[3, 23]By offloading the active working set from the JVM heap to off-heap/native memory and spilling larger states to local disks (such as NVMe/SSD), RocksDB avoids JVM Garbage Collection pauses and supports states larger than the allocated memory footprint.[4, 23]The native memory allocation is managed by Flink's managed memory allocator.[23, 27] The allocator provisions a shared write buffer manager and block cache across all embedded RocksDB instances within a task slot.[27]Active writes are buffered in memory within mutable write buffers (MemTables).[28] Once a MemTable reaches its configured limit, it is converted into an immutable MemTable and flushed to local SSDs as sorted string tables (SSTables) organized in a multi-level hierarchy.[28, 29] Hot data is cached in the off-heap block cache to minimize disk reads.[28, 29]Tuning RocksDB requires balancing memory allocation between write buffers, block caches, and JVM overhead.[6, 27] If write buffers are sized too small, frequent flushes trigger background compactions, causing write amplification and compaction stalls.[4, 29] Conversely, if the block cache is under-allocated, frequent disk reads of index and filter blocks degrade read performance.[27, 29]The write amplification factor of this process can be modeled as:WA = \frac{B_{disk}}{B_{mem}}where B_{disk} is the total number of bytes written to local disk storage, and B_{mem} is the number of bytes written to the active MemTable. High write amplification degrades SSD durability and introduces disk I/O bottlenecks.[4]To optimize index lookups, Flink configures Bloom filters at the SSTable level, allowing RocksDB to skip reading files that do not contain a requested key.[29] For state cleanup, Flink provides a compaction filter that runs during background compactions, evaluating and removing expired keys based on State Time-To-Live (TTL) configurations without overhead on the main processing path.[4, 29]To minimize network and storage I/O during checkpoints, RocksDB utilizes incremental checkpointing.[3, 4] Because SSTables are immutable once flushed to disk, Flink's incremental checkpointing engine tracks which SST files have been created or modified since the last successful checkpoint.[3, 28] During a checkpoint, only these new SST files are uploaded to the remote checkpoint storage (e.g., S3 or GCS).[3, 28] Unchanged files are referenced via metadata pointers, reducing the transfer volume of large states to the incremental delta.[3]RocksDB was upgraded from version 5.17 to 6.20 in Flink 1.14, introducing finer-grained write buffer memory control, deleteRange interface, and ARM platform support.[27]5.2.3.2.1.3. Two-Phase Commit Sinks: Transaction, Consensus, and Exactly-OnceFor applications requiring end-to-end exactly-once semantics, Flink relies on a Two-Phase Commit (2PC) protocol implemented through the abstract TwoPhaseCommitSinkFunction.[12, 14, 17] This utility coordinates commits and rollbacks of external systems in alignment with Flink's checkpointing lifecycle, requiring that the target sink system supports transactions.[12, 17, 30] The TwoPhaseCommitSinkFunction implements Flink's CheckpointedFunction and CheckpointListener interfaces, utilizing two state descriptors to track transactions across failures: pendingTransactions (which manages the active, uncommitted transactions) and pendingCommitTransactions (which maps completed checkpoint IDs to their pre-committed transactions).[31]The transactional lifecycle operates through several distinct methods:beginTransaction(): Invoked when a new checkpoint cycle starts.[17, 33] The sink initializes a new transaction handle (e.g., opening a temporary file or starting a database transaction).[17]invoke(): Processes incoming records by writing them to the active transaction.[31, 33] These writes are staged but remain uncommitted and invisible to downstream readers.[17, 30]preCommit(): Triggered when a checkpoint barrier reaches the sink operator.[17, 33] The sink flushes and closes the active transaction, ensuring all written data is physically persisted in the external system's transaction log.[17, 31, 32] The transaction handle is then saved into Flink's checkpointed state under the pendingCommitTransactions map.[31] Immediately after, a new transaction is initiated via beginTransaction() for subsequent records.[17]commit(): Executed asynchronously when the JobManager receives successful checkpoint acknowledgments from all tasks and notifies the sink via the CheckpointListener.[17, 31, 34] The sink commits the specific pre-committed transaction, making the staged data visible downstream.[17, 33] Once committed, the transaction is removed from the pending state.[31]abort(): Triggered if a checkpoint fails, is canceled, or times out.[31, 34] The sink aborts the transaction and purges the associated temporary files or database transaction logs, rolling back all uncommitted writes.[17]During recovery from a task failure, Flink restores the sink's state from the last successful checkpoint.[12, 17] Since the failure may have occurred after the checkpoint completed but before the commit notification reached the sink, the restored state may contain unresolved pre-committed transactions.[17]To handle this, the TwoPhaseCommitSinkFunction executes recoverAndCommit() on all transactions found in pendingCommitTransactions, forcing a preemptive commit.[31] If a transaction was not pre-committed before the failure, the operator calls recoverAndAbort() to clean up dangling transactions.[31] Because commits are retried upon failure, the sink's commit phase must be idempotent to prevent duplicate data exposure in the target system.[17]
5.2.3.2.2. RisingWave: SQL, Streaming, and MaterializationRisingWave is designed as a distributed SQL streaming database built in Rust, adopting a cloud-native, disaggregated compute-storage architecture.[1, 2, 8] Unlike traditional stream processing frameworks, RisingWave decouples computing, storage, and catalog metadata, storing its persistent state directly in cloud object storage.[2, 6]5.2.3.2.2.1. Epoch-Based Snapshots: Consistency, Timeline, and IsolationRisingWave maintains consistency across its processing timeline through an epoch-based barrier protocol.[6, 7, 24] The Meta Node manages this protocol by periodically injecting synchronization barriers (typically every 1 second) into the streaming data sources.[6, 24] These barriers establish epoch boundaries, partitioning the streaming timeline into discrete, sequentially incrementing epochs.[6, 24]As barriers flow through the streaming DAG, operators flush their state changes associated with that epoch from memory to Hummock, creating an immutable state snapshot on object storage.[6, 7] Once all downstream operators process the barrier and acknowledge that their state has been persisted, the Meta Node registers the epoch as committed.[7, 24]This epoch-based model supports snapshot isolation for user-initiated queries.[1, 7] When a client executes a batch query on a materialized view, the Serving Node pins the query's view to the latest committed epoch, filtering out any uncommitted updates from the active epoch.[6, 7, 24]This guarantees consistent snapshots for any data access, ensuring that ad-hoc queries return complete results without dirty reads or partial transaction anomalies.[1] The streaming engine also guarantees eventual consistency across cascading materialized views, where one view reads from another, by synchronizing updates along the shared epoch timeline.[2, 6, 21]5.2.3.2.2.2. Disaggregated State Storage: Computing, Scaling, and SeparationRisingWave's architecture decouples computing, storage, and catalog metadata, allowing each component to scale independently in response to varying workloads.[2, 6, 25] This disaggregation is managed across four dedicated node types [24, 25]:Serving Nodes: Act as stateless frontends compatible with the PostgreSQL wire protocol.[24, 25] They handle client connections, parse and bind incoming SQL queries, optimize execution plans, and serve ad-hoc batch queries with low latency.[24, 25] Batch queries can run in local mode (executing simple point-lookups on a single node) or distributed mode (orchestrating complex parallel joins across the compute layer).[24, 25]Compute Nodes: Form the streaming execution layer.[24, 25] They run stateless streaming operators (such as actors in a directed graph) that perform continuous incremental computations.[24] These nodes partition data streams using virtual nodes (vnodes), allowing tasks to scale horizontally across the cluster without data sharding issues.[24] Compute nodes maintain an in-memory cache of active state but write all persistent mutations downstream.[6, 7]Compactor Nodes: Dedicated background workers that optimize storage efficiency and read latency.[7, 24, 25] They pull fragmented, unsorted state files from object storage, merge and sort them, and write compacted, organized files back.[7, 24] This isolates the CPU-intensive compaction process from the active streaming ingestion path, preventing latency spikes.[8, 24]Meta Nodes: Serve as the central coordinator and catalog manager.[7, 24, 25] They track cluster topology, schedule streaming fragments, orchestrate checkpoint boundaries, manage Hummock storage metadata, and assign compaction tasks to compactor nodes.[7, 24, 25]This separation of concerns simplifies scaling.[2, 5] When scaling a Flink cluster, the local state files must be physically redistributed and copied among the TaskManagers, which can take several minutes for large states.[2, 6]In RisingWave, scaling is a metadata-only operation known as schema-based rescaling.[5] When compute nodes are added or removed, the Meta Node updates Hummock's metadata mapping of virtual nodes to compute nodes.[5, 6, 7] The newly provisioned compute nodes point directly to the existing SST files on shared object storage and begin processing, avoiding physical state transfers and enabling fast scaling.[2, 5, 6]5.2.3.2.2.3. Cloud-Native Storage Engine: S3, Latency, and CachingAt the core of RisingWave's state management is Hummock, an LSM-tree storage engine designed for remote object storage.[7, 9, 10] Hummock uses highly available cloud object storage (such as AWS S3, Google Cloud Storage, or Azure Blob Storage) as its primary persistent store.[7, 9, 10]To interact with these backends, Hummock leverages the OpenDAL library, which abstracts the API variations of different cloud providers.[8] By using object storage instead of provisioning high-performance local SSDs on compute nodes, Hummock can lower storage costs.[6, 7, 8]However, remote object storage introduces high read latencies and request-based pricing.[9] To mitigate these performance and cost trade-offs, RisingWave integrates Foyer, a hybrid memory-and-disk caching engine.[9] Foyer utilizes both JVM-free RAM and local NVMe/SSD disks on compute nodes as a unified cache, expanding the active working set cache capacity.[9]Foyer addresses three main storage challenges:Working Set Overflow: Large-state joins and high-cardinality aggregations often exceed a node's physical memory, forcing frequent reads from remote object storage.[9] Foyer uses local disk space to cache hot state blocks, preventing frequent round-trips to remote object storage and maintaining read throughput close to local-disk architectures.[9]Cache Invalidation from Compaction: Background compactions merge older SSTables into new ones, invalidating old cached SSTable references and causing cache misses.[9] Foyer resolves this with a compaction-aware cache refill mechanism.[9] When a compaction task completes, the compute node refills the newly generated SSTables into the Foyer cache before updating the active catalog manifest.[9] To prevent hot cache jitter, these refilled SSTables are placed directly into the local disk cache rather than the memory cache.[9]S3 Access Costs and Fragmentation: Random read access patterns require balancing the transfer block size.[9] Small blocks increase the number of GET requests, raising cloud access costs; large blocks reduce request counts but cause memory fragmentation.[9] Because Foyer's disk cache can be sized significantly larger than memory, RisingWave can use larger block sizes.[9] Refill requests pull dozens to thousands of consecutive blocks in a single, large GET request, utilizing S3's high sequential throughput while reducing overall API billing costs.[9]To manage writes and compactions, Hummock coordinates resource scaling between compute and compaction services.[24] As data ingestion rates scale, write volume increases, creating a backlog of uncompacted SSTables in L0.[24] This accumulation of files can degrade read performance because of the need to merge-sort across multiple fragmented runs.[24]To prevent this, RisingWave supports serverless compaction on its managed cloud platform, dynamically scaling compactor nodes to match write volume and keep L0 small and well-sorted.[24] The allocation of compute-to-compaction capacity can be expressed as:R = \frac{C_p}{C_c}where C_p is the compute node CPU allocation, and C_c is the compactor node CPU allocation. For standard workloads, a ratio of R = 2:1 (e.g., a 4-core compute node paired with a 2-core compactor) is sufficient to maintain storage health.[24] For write-heavy pipelines, such as historical data backfills, the ratio is adjusted to R = 1:8, scaling the compactor resources to prevent compaction bottlenecks.[24]5.2.3.2.2.4. SQL-Native State: Tables, Queries, and LogicIn RisingWave, state is defined implicitly through standard PostgreSQL-compatible SQL queries.[2, 6, 19] When a user creates a materialized view, RisingWave automatically determines the execution plan, instantiates the required streaming actors, and maintains the backing state on Hummock.[6, 24] This SQL-native state mapping matches RisingWave's supported data types with standard PostgreSQL representations.[19, 20]By using a SQL-native interface, RisingWave simplifies several common stream processing patterns:State Expiry: Flink requires developers to manually configure state TTL policies on state descriptors, which can be error-prone.[16, 28, 29] RisingWave manages state cleanup through temporal filters and window specifications in the SQL query.[19] It automatically prunes state outside of temporal join boundaries or tumbling window intervals.[6, 19]Complex Event Processing (CEP): Flink SQL uses the SQL-standard MATCH_RECOGNIZE clause to support pattern matching over event sequences.[2, 19] RisingWave does not support MATCH_RECOGNIZE or custom procedural Java operators.[2, 5] Instead, it handles pattern-matching use cases through materialized views, temporal filters, and window aggregations, which can be more readable for streaming SQL users.[19]Data Pipelines: RisingWave supports cascading materialized views, allowing users to build multi-stage pipelines where one view reads directly from another.[2, 21] This allows complex ETL pipelines to be expressed and run entirely within the database.[2]
5.2.3.3. Strategic Deployment Framework: Operational and Architectural DecisivenessSelecting between Apache Flink and RisingWave depends on an organization's existing skills, processing requirements, and operational constraints.Scenarios Favoring Apache FlinkApache Flink remains a suitable option for organizations with the following requirements:Custom Procedural Logic: Flink's low-level DataStream API provides fine-grained control over state allocation, timer registration, and side outputs.[2, 5, 6] If a pipeline requires custom graph topologies or procedural logic that cannot be expressed in standard declarative SQL, Flink's programmatic model is well-suited.[2, 5]Complex Pattern Matching: For applications that rely on pattern matching over sequential events, Flink's mature CEP library and native support for the MATCH_RECOGNIZE clause provide a robust, out-of-the-box solution.[2, 19]Vast Connector Integration: If a pipeline must ingest from or write to legacy systems, mainframes, or specialized database engines, Flink's ecosystem of over 100 pre-built connectors provides broad integration options.[2]In-Memory Low Latency: For applications with small state requirements that require sub-millisecond end-to-end processing latency, Flink's HashMapStateBackend keeps all state as Java objects on the JVM heap.[3, 28, 29] This avoids the serialization and disk I/O overhead of a database storage layer, delivering fast processing speeds for workloads that fit entirely in memory.[3, 28, 29]Scenarios Favoring RisingWaveRisingWave is a strong architectural choice for organizations prioritizing the following goals:SQL-First Analytics: If the data platform team consists primarily of SQL developers, data engineers, or analysts, RisingWave eliminates the need to manage Java-based stream processing frameworks.[2, 6] Defining pipelines as cascading SQL materialized views lowers the barrier to entry and speeds up development.[2, 6]Simplified Data Architecture: RisingWave acts as both a stream processor and a serving database.[1, 2] By storing state internally and serving queries directly via the PostgreSQL wire protocol, it removes the operational complexity of managing external downstream databases and maintaining cross-system consistency.[1, 2, 6]Low Operational Overhead: For teams without dedicated platform engineers, RisingWave's disaggregated, S3-native architecture simplifies cluster management.[2, 5] There are no local SSD disks to provision, no complex RocksDB parameters to tune, and upgrades or scaling operations do not require managing manual savepoints or state transfers.[2, 5, 6]Cost-Effective Large-State Storage: For workloads with large streaming states (ranging from tens of gigabytes to several terabytes), RisingWave's S3-native storage model reduces infrastructure costs.[2, 5, 9] By shifting state storage from expensive local SSDs to cheap cloud object storage and utilizing Foyer's hybrid disk caching to preserve read performance, RisingWave can lower total infrastructure costs compared to coupled-storage Flink clusters.[2, 8, 9]Strict Recovery SLAs: If the business requires sub-minute recovery times for large-state applications, RisingWave's metadata-driven recovery is a key differentiator.[2] Compute nodes can crash and recover in seconds because they do not need to download or rebuild local database files before resuming processing.[2, 6]

Source: RisingWave architecture - RisingWave
URL: https://docs.risingwave.com/get-started/architecture
RisingWave architecture - RisingWave Skip to main content RisingWave home page Current (v2.8) Search... Ctrl K Ask Assistant Blog Sign up free Sign up free Search...
